0756405890|5.0|First let me start by saying that I am well versed in the fantasy genre.  Some of my favorites include GRRM's Song of Ice and Fire Series, Robin Hobbs (Farseer and Tawny Man Series), the Mists of Avalon, the Harry Potter Books, and All of Tolkien but particularly The Hobbit.  Others I have read and enjoyed but would not group in with the first batch are Terry Goodkind, Robert Jordan, David Farland, Anne Bishop, Raymond Feist and Lynn Flewelling.    In terms of books I recommend to others - both Fantasy readers and first timers - The Name of the Wind is at the top of my list.  One of the main reasons for these recommendations is the quality of the writing.  There is a heartbreaking beauty and lyricism to his writing that is evident from the very first page. It is a book teeming with genuine emotion, melodic imagination and compelling characters. Rothfuss can take something common place and find nuances that not only bring the object or situation to life but give it a vivid new life.  I enjoyed the language of this book so much that I have re-read it several times.  I find that even people who do not read fantasy books as a rule enjoy this one because of the writing.    Secondly, I found the characters in this book to be intriguing and exciting.  The main character is type of hero I love - a flawed overachiever struggling to make his way in the world.  But I also loved reading about Ben, Kvothe's Parents, Auri (a personal favorite), Elodin, Bast, and Fela.  In fact, with only one exception (Kvothe's love interest) I found that the writing gave excellent insight into the minds and motives of the characters.      I also found the concept of magic in this book very intriguing.  The idea of naming (knowing something so completely that you understand its secret core name) as a powerful magic source felt different and exciting to me.  I also enjoyed the acknowledgment that much of the \\"magic\\" perceived by the uneducated is actually a study of chemistry or physics in Kvothe's world as well as ours.  A well educated mind is the key to magic in this world rather than an inborn talent or a magic wand.      Some reviews have criticized the pace or plot of this novel.  I found both to be just fine for me.  I felt that both the sense of a driving emotional force present throughout the book and the sequence of the plot events kept things moving at a great pace.  For me this book was a page turner that I did not want to put down.  The book also contains a number of universal themes such as loss and coming of age which help add to it's appeal.    All in all - this is one of my favorite books and I am greatly looking forward to book two.  I hope others enjoy it as much as I have.  All of the people I have gifted copies to certainly have enjoyed it.     (I would also like to state for the record that I am in no way affiliated with the author, publisher, or anyone else who makes money off this book.  I am just a fantasy reader who found a book she loved.  Thanks!)

B001KYJA6K|5.0|I have become obsessed with this disc.  After one hearing, I decided it was great iPod material and imported it right away, and I just keep listening.  These performers understand that Monteverdi was one of the great songwriters and most popular composers of his time, and that it was a time that valued improvisation and imagination in the performance of music.  They supply the imagination in spades!  They even provide some \\"swing\\" to a few of the songs, with no announcement in the booklet that this will be happening.  When I played this for a friend whose sole exposure to Monteverdi so far was the 1610 Vespers, he said \\"No way, that's not Monteverdi.\\"  But it IS Monteverdi.  I especially enjoyed Philippe Jaroussky's work in two solo songs and two excerpts from The Coronation of Poppea. Nuria Rial is splendid in selections from the late madrigal books.  Pluhar and her ensemble play Monteverdi like the popular composer that he was. The imaginative accompaniments are faithful to the originals but take off in delightful directions.  I wish they would record a follow-up - there is so much great Monteverdi material of this type, and they've but scratched the surface!

The rest of my original comments, below, still stand.<br /><br />___ ORIGINAL REVIEW ___<br /><br />\\"The Hero of Ages\\" is the third in Brandon Sanderson's \\"Mistborn\\" series. It pretty much exhibits the same traits as the previous two volumes (amazingly creative universe and magic system, good technical writing, not-so-great character development, and TOO MANY WORDS). So, if you liked those books, you should be pretty happy with this one. But, if you were hoping the series would get better as it progressed, well, you'll probably be disappointed. From my capitalized phrase, above, you can guess that my biggest complaint is that, just like the second book, fully the first two thirds of the book is just filler (it's a bit better than what's in the earlier book since the filler at least has some action occurring, even if it's busy work). And, once again, the last third is the very good part where everything happens. Again, if Sanderson had just condensed those first 400 pages down to 100 pages, the book would have been great.
